# Chapter 1 Markov Decision Processes of a Two-Tier Supply Chain Inventory System

![](images/3ca1fa87e41319cdadf01aa3d6db1e1212acc695130d75d983442567c54f6b1f.jpg)

R. Sivasamy, Dinesh K. Sharma, and Keamogetse Setlhare

Abstract Our supply chain (SC) model is based on a two-tier queueing-inventory system. This facility delivers packages of $\mathrm { Q } > 0$ fixed-size items from its inventory stored in a distribution centre (DC) to a retail shopping mall (RSM). This RSM processes customer requirements through an $\mathbf { M } / \mathbf { M } _ { 1 } + \mathbf { M } _ { 2 } / 1 / \mathbf { K } .$ -rule service facility. Requests to the RSM node follow the Poisson process with the parameter (λ). The inventory guideline applied at node RSM is of type (0, Q), and the order of most items $\mathrm { Q } > 0$ is placed on the DC when inventory decreases from Q to level 0 with a threshold guideline called f-policy. Thus, at the end of the delay, the DC output of the quantity ordered by RSM is exponentially distributed with the parameter γ $( \gamma > 0 )$ . For each request to the RSM node, an article is only available after the exponential service time with the parameter μ $( \mu > 0 )$ . The waiting room at the commercial node has unlimited capacity. This SC management (SCM) is called a queue inventory system with a $\operatorname { f } _ { 1 } - \operatorname { p o l i c y } .$ The common stochastic process $\mathbf { Z ( t ) = }$ (N(t), I(t)) to link the number of customers demands available at time $\cdot _ { \mathrm { { t } } } ,$ by N(t) and the on-hand inventory level by I(t) is formulated. Because of the properties of the exponential distributions and of the $\operatorname { f } _ { 1 } - \operatorname { p o l i c y } ,$ , this pair-sequence Z(t) forms a Markov decision process (MDP) that changes its states with the passage of times $\cdot _ { \mathrm { { t } } } ,$ . The Z-systems’ stationary properties are checked at service completion. We also use the linear function of total expected cost to determine the optimal order quantity and optimal order patterns. Using the same methodology, the analysis is extended by assuming that the two processes are independent under a new policy $\mathrm { f } _ { 2 }$ . Numerical illustrations are also provided to support the results obtained from the proposed MDP approach.

R. Sivasamy (B) K. Setlhare

Department of Statistics, Faculty of Social Sciences, University of Botswana,

Gaborone, Botswana

e-mail: sivasamyr@ub.ac.bw

K. Setlhare

e-mail: setlhark@ub.ac.bw

D. K. Sharma

University of Maryland Eastern Shore, Princess Anne, MD 21804, USA

e-mail: dksharma@umes.edu

Keywords Supply chain · Service facility · f1-policy · f2-policy · K-policy · Markov decision process · Stationary distribution · Expected total cost · Optimum order quantity

# 1.1 Markov Decision Processes of a Supply Chain

A supply chain (SC) is a system that monitors the constant flow of products (cars, televisions, etc.) between different wholesale and retail vendors and within their subdepartments and collects revenue from each of them. The client or customer is an integral part of the SC everywhere. The main objective is to satisfy the customer’s needs and therefore generate the maximum profit for our SC.

The key task for success is the integration of sales branches into a supply chain that would improve customer satisfaction and loyalty economically. Several SC researchers (Dey and Giri 2014; Hsien-Jen 2013; Lin 2009) have used integrated policy to study the effects of integration between a seller and a buyer, as it helps reduce lead times and inventory costs. This task not only helps identify problem areas and inform wise decisions but also reduces the costs and improves the final expected revenues.

This book chapter discusses an integration method between inventory and queue models that allow requirements to occur during the inventory period that are only processed after the ordered items have arrived.

An example is a wholesale sales office (for example headquarters) selling cars electronics consumer products (TVs refrigerators etc.) and providing orders for one or more middle retail sales centres. After that each shopping centre can serve customer requirements using some queue discipline like the FCFS (First Come First Served) and inventory policy like the (s, S), (r, Q) etc., to maximize their ends.

Markov decision making focuses on sequential decision making under conditions of uncertainty. It would also represent a discrete stochastic process under the partial control of an outside observer. During each transition period, the state of use of the process is observed and based on this observation the controller selects an action “a” that affects the use of the system in the next moment. In addition, the observer receives a reward r (a, s) or costs c (a, s) for each step, depending on the selected action “a” and the state of the system at “s”. The key components of this model are states and transition probabilities in the action space.

The main objective is how the observer should act to maximize the reward that may materialize subject to certain restrictions on the permissible states of the system. The objective is, therefore, to find decision rules that specify the action to be carried out at a given moment, as well as guidelines that consist of a series of decision rules.

A simple approach is to focus on the properties of the MDP, whose future states and reward or cost depend on the number of available shares and the transition probabilities in each period from the original state to the current state. To answer the question on “When is the optimal policy?”, We will build and propose the optimal criteria and the shape of the base model.

Several estimation methods will be used to find service providers in mathematical models, including service queue interactions and inventory control. For example, Berman and Kim (1999) studied the behaviour of service systems associated with an inventory. Inventory templates for ongoing review have become popular as computerized inventory control systems have evolved in recent years. Customers arriving during the inventory period contribute to the constant evaluation of inventory samples, leading to loss of revenue (Daduna and Szekli 2006).

Many retail businesses inevitably experience stagnation as stiff competition can force customers to change brands or locations. To support this type of switching phenomenon, a pure inventory model is described (Mohebbi and Posner 1998).

Further sections of this chapter are organized as follows: Sect. 1.2 deals with a DC attached to a RSM via an $\mathbf { M } / \mathbf { M } _ { 1 } + \mathbf { M } _ { 2 } / 1$ /K-rule facility operating under a deterministic (0, Q) inventory policy and obtained steady state characteristics of the inventory cum queuing management. We present in Sect. 1.3 the analysis of the problem concerning performance mmeasures and compute numerical results for the proposed system and thus form the core of the chapter. The results for M/M/1/FCFS branch under the same f-policy are deduced in Sects. 1.4 and 1.6 provides conclusions and future scope.

# 1.2 M/M1 + M2/1 /K-Rule/f1-policy Queues + Inventory Model with Backorders

We present a SC system that integrates $\mathrm { M } / \mathrm { M } _ { 1 } + \mathrm { M } _ { 2 } / 1 / \mathrm { K } \mathrm { - r u l e }$ queue under the FCFS discipline with an inventory management, continuous review, and exponential lead time evaluation results from a prior M/M/1 system, for which Schwarz and Daduna (2006) examined under a deterministic (0, Q) order policy with a threshold $\cdot _ { 1 } \cdot$ . In the proposed SC model, we have integrated a two-tier inventory system: a distribution centre (DC) that delivers $\mathrm { Q } > 0$ items from the stocked warehouse to the second linked home, i.e., a retail shopping mall (RSM) that operates with an $\mathbf { M } / \mathbf { M } _ { 1 } + \mathbf { M } _ { 2 } / 1 / \mathbf { K } .$ -rule equipment.

An inventory policy is applied on this RSM node of type (0, Q) where $\mathrm { Q } > 0$ item orders are placed with DC when inventory levels go from Q level to zero while there is at least one request in the queue waiting. The delay time in DC for the size ordered by RSM is exponentially distributed with the parameter $( \gamma > 0 )$ ). Whenever the server is free to serve a customer and inventory is zero, this service will start when the next replenishment arrives while the customers who arrive during this replenishment phase are added to the backorder list. As the higher the number of items backordered, the higher the demand for the item and so such backorders are an important factor in this integrated SCM analysis.

Suppose that a non-queue observer monitors our SC system and ensures that the service times are exponentially distributed with a mean service time of $( 1 / \mu )$ and the queue length range is (0, K]. Alternately, if the queue length range is [(K

$+ 1 ) , \infty )$ , a temporary server is installed to join with the permanent one to boost the exponential service rate to $( \mu + \mu _ { 1 } ) , \mu _ { 1 } > 0$ . Each arriving customer demands exactly one item from the inventory which passes through a homogenous Poisson process with the demand rate λ. We assume that sequences of service times, inter-arrival times, and lead times are mutually independent, and all random times within those sequences are identically distributed.

Our primary focus is on the bi-variate sequence $\mathbf { Z } ( \mathrm { t } ) = \left( \mathrm { N } ( \mathrm { t } ) , \mathrm { I } ( \mathrm { t } ) \right)$ , where N(t) $= { \mathrm { q u e u e } }$ length and $\operatorname { I } ( \mathrm { t } ) = \mathrm { o n }$ hand inventory level at time ‘t’. It can be shown that the sequence $\mathbf { Z ( t ) }$ forms a Markov process under the continuous review inventory policy (0, Q) with a threshold level $( \mathrm { N } ( \mathrm { t } ) = \mathrm { n } > 0 , \mathrm { I } ( \mathrm { t } ) = 0 )$ for placing an order. Here, the number of items is sold out at the current service rate (if available) while the customer is waiting for service only.

Thus, this book chapter provides a solution to the problem of finding performance metrics and the optimal order quantity $\mathbf { Q } ^ { * }$ for the SC model under consideration.

Main Issues: How does inventory ‘f’ policy react to customer queues at the service centre? How are traditional queue length, waiting times, etc., influenced by attached inventory policy?, and vice versa.

Inventory policy $\mathbf { \sqrt { 6 6 7 } } \mathbf { \sqrt { 7 } } \mathbf { \sqrt { 3 } } .$ The inventory policy for the current study is the policy $\cdot _ { \mathrm { f _ { 1 } } }$ $\mathbf { \Omega } = ( 0 , \mathbf { Q } ) \mathbf { \Omega }$ with a queue length threshold of $" 1 "$ as the decision variable. There can be at most one order, with 0 being the order point, and exactly a bulk of Q units arrive with each replenishment. According to guidelines of the $\mathrm { \cdot f _ { 1 } }$ ’ policy subject to its threshold value 1, an order is triggered when inventory is empty and there is at least one customer in the system. For more details on the same type of inventory management associated with a queue system that depletes inventory at the server’s service rate (and higher than the customer demand rate) during non-empty queue time, see (Schwarz and Daduna 2006).

$\mathbf { E _ { f } }$ state space: Let $\mathbf { N } _ { 0 } = \{ 0 , 1 , 2 . . . . \}$ and $\mathbf { Q } _ { 0 } = \{ 0 , 1 , 2 , . . . , \mathrm { Q } \}$ be the state spaces of the queue length process N(t) and that of the Inventory process I(t) respectively. If inventory policy $\cdot _ { \mathrm { f _ { 1 } } } ,$ and queue policy $\mathbf { \hat { K } } \mathbf { \hat { \Sigma } }$ work together, the queue-inventory (N(t), I(t)) does not reach the state (0, Q) because the replenishment order is not triggered if there are no clients on the server. However, to simplify the notation, we use the Cartesian product state space $\mathrm { E } _ { \mathrm { f } } = \mathbf { N } _ { 0 } \times \mathbf { Q } _ { 0 }$ and find that all formulas for this state (0, Q) must specify exactly zero stationary accessibility.

Service and ordering methods: Whenever the server is free to serve a customer and inventory is zero, this service will start when the next replenishment arrives. This means that customers who arrive during this replenishment phase when inventory is empty are added to the backorder list. More recent studies in integrated models have addressed the problem of how classical performance measures (e.g., queue length, waiting time, etc.) are influenced by the management of attached inventory and vice versa.

# 1.2.1 The Stable M/M1 + M/1 /K-rule/f1-policy Model

The time dependent Markov process (N (t), I (t)) serves as a Markov Decision process on the state space $\mathrm { E _ { f } }$ in the sequel. Let the random vector of the stationary state process be denoted by $Z = ( { \mathrm { N } } , \operatorname { I } ) = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { t \to \infty } ( { \mathrm { N } } ( { \mathrm { t } } ) , \operatorname { I } ( { \mathrm { t } } ) )$ . We denote this integrated type of queueing-inventory system by $\mathrm { M } / \mathrm { M } _ { 1 } + \mathrm { M } _ { 2 } / 1 / \mathrm { K } \mathrm { - r u l e } / \mathrm { f } _ { 1 } \mathrm { - p o l i c y } .$ .

# 1.2.1.1 Generator Matrix of the Inventory Process I When $\mathbf { K } > \mathbf { Q }$

The standard probabilistic arguments can establish the limiting process Z be distributed according to the equilibrium of the $\mathbf { M } / \mathbf { M } _ { 1 } + \mathbf { M } _ { 2 } / 1 / \mathbf { K } \mathbf { - r u l e } / \mathbf { f } _ { 1 } \mathbf { - p o l i c y }$ . Since we assume that $\mathbf { Q } < \infty$ , it can be shown that the process $\textsf { Z }$ is regular and irreducible in the space $[ \mathrm { E } _ { \mathrm { f } } - ( 0 , \mathrm { Q } ) ]$ . Therefore, Z is a quasi-birth- and-death (QBD) process with a generating matrix between levels A of order $( \mathsf Q + 1 ) \mathrm { { x } } ( \mathsf Q + 1 ) \mathrm { { x } } ( \mathsf Q ) =$ :

$$
\boldsymbol {A} = \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \\ \vdots \\ Q \end{array} \left( \begin{array}{c c c c c c} - \gamma & 0 & 0 & \dots & \dots & \gamma \\ \mu & - \mu & 0 & 0 & \dots & 0 \\ 0 & \mu & - \mu & 0 & \dots & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & \mu & - \mu & \ddots & \vdots \\ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \ddots & \ddots & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & \dots & 0 & \mu & - \mu \end{array} \right) \tag {1.1}
$$

We see that the matrix A serves as the infinitesimal generator of a finite, irreducible, continuous time Markov process $\mathbf { I } = \operatorname* { l i m } _ { t \to \infty } \mathbf { I } ( t )$ governing the transition states/changes of the DC if $\mu , \gamma > 0$ t→∞ with stationary distribution ${ \pmb { \alpha } } = ( \alpha _ { 0 } , \alpha _ { 1 } , . . . ,$ $\alpha _ { \mathrm { Q } } )$ , where $\alpha _ { 0 } + \alpha _ { 1 } + \ldots + \alpha _ { 0 } = 1$ . Denote the unit vector of size (Q 1) by ${ \bf e } =$ $( 1 , 1 , . . . , 1 )$ . Then,

$$
\left(\alpha_ {0}, \alpha_ {1}, \dots , \alpha_ {\mathrm {Q}}\right) \mathbf {A} = \mathbf {0}, \left(\alpha_ {0}, \alpha_ {1}, \dots , \alpha_ {\mathrm {Q}}\right) \mathbf {e} = 1 \tag {1.2}
$$

The unique solution for α of (1.2) is given in (1.3):

$$
\alpha_ {0} = \frac {\mu}{\mu + Q \gamma}, \text {a n d} \alpha_ {\mathrm {j}} = \frac {\gamma}{\mu + Q \gamma} \text {f o r} j = 1, \dots , Q \tag {1.3}
$$

By replacing the service rate μ by $\mu _ { 2 } = \left( \mu + \mu _ { 1 } \right)$ in the matrix generator A, the DC can obtain the inter-level distribution, say $\alpha ^ { * } = ( \alpha _ { 0 } ^ { * } , \alpha _ { _ 1 } ^ { * } , \ldots , \alpha _ { 0 } ^ { * } )$ to deliver goods to the RSM when the queue length $\mathbf { \ddot { N } } \in \{ ( \mathbf { K } + 1 ) , ( \mathbf { K } + 2 ) , \ldots \} ^ { \mathrm { , } }$ at any time of the completion of the service. By solving for the vector of stationary distributions $\alpha _ { \mathrm { j } } \ \mathrm { f o r } j = 1 , . . . , \mathrm { Q }$ , and for $\alpha ^ { * } = ( \alpha _ { 0 } ^ { * } , \ : \alpha _ { _ 1 } ^ { * } , \ : . \ : . , \ : \alpha _ { 0 } ^ { * } )$ we get

$$
\alpha_ {0} ^ {*} = \frac {\left(\mu + \mu_ {1}\right)}{\left(\mu + \mu_ {1}\right) + Q \gamma}, \quad \text {a n d} \quad \alpha_ {j} ^ {*} = \frac {\gamma}{\left(\mu + \mu_ {1}\right) + Q \gamma} \quad \text {f o r} j = 1, \dots , Q \tag {1.4}
$$

We want to find out how the two alternating distributions $\{ \alpha _ { \mathrm { j } } \}$ and $\{ { \alpha } _ { \mathrm { j } } ^ { * } \}$ play a role in finding the optimal value for Q (i.e. the optimal order quantity $\mathbf { Q } ^ { * } )$ in our discussions.

# 1.2.1.2 Generator Matrix of the QBD Process Z When $\mathbf { K } > \mathbf { Q }$

Since the Z process is a level-dependent QBD process, using the properties of the QBD in terms of the K-policy and the f-policy of the inventory process, we get an infinitesimal generating matrix $\mathbf { R } = ( \mathbf { r } _ { \mathrm { ( L i , L j ) } } )$ , where $\mathrm { L _ { i } } = ( \mathrm { ( i , 0 ) } , \mathrm { ( i , 1 ) } , \mathrm { ~ } . . . ( \mathrm { i , } 0 ) )$ i s called the $\mathrm { i ^ { \mathrm { t h } } }$ level for $\mathrm { i } \in  { \mathrm { N } } _ { 0 }$ , and $\textstyle \bigcup _ { i = 0 } ^ { \infty } L _ { i } = E _ { f }$ as follows, where each entry is a square matrix of order $( \mathsf { Q } + 1 )$ :

$$
\mathbf {R} = \begin{array}{c} L _ {1} \\ L _ {2} \\ \vdots \\ L _ {K - 1} \\ L _ {K} \\ L _ {K + 2} \\ \vdots \\ \vdots \\ \vdots \end{array} \left( \begin{array}{l l l l l l l l l l} - A _ {0} & A _ {0} & 0 & \dots & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & \dots \\ A _ {2} & A _ {1} & A _ {0} & \ddots & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & \dots \\ 0 & A _ {2} & A _ {1} & \ddots & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & \dots \\ \vdots & \ddots & \ddots & \ddots & \ddots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots \\ 0 & 0 & 0 & \ddots & A _ {1} & A _ {0} & 0 & 0 & 0 & \dots \\ 0 & 0 & 0 & \ddots & A _ {2} & A _ {1} & A _ {0} & 0 & 0 & \ddots \\ 0 & 0 & 0 & \dots & 0 & A _ {2} & A _ {1} & A _ {0} & 0 & \ddots \\ 0 & 0 & 0 & \dots & 0 & 0 & A _ {4} & A _ {3} & A _ {0} & \ddots \\ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \ddots & 0 & 0 & 0 & A _ {4} & A _ {3} & \ddots \\ \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & A _ {4} & \ddots \\ \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \dots & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & \ddots & \ddots \end{array} \right) \tag {1.5}
$$

Here, explicit forms of all non-zero square matrices of order $( \mathsf { Q } + 1 )$ of the generator R are given: $\mathbf { A } _ { 0 } = \mathrm { d i a g } ( \lambda )$ i.e. a diagonal matrix,

$$
A _ {0} = \left( \begin{array}{l l l l l} \lambda & 0 & \dots & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & \lambda & \dots & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & \ddots & \vdots & 0 \\ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \ddots & \vdots \\ 0 & \dots & \dots & 0 & \lambda \end{array} \right), A _ {2} ^ {(\mu)} = \left( \begin{array}{l l l l l} 0 & 0 & \dots & 0 & 0 \\ \mu & 0 & \dots & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & \mu & 0 & \vdots & 0 \\ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \ddots & \vdots \\ 0 & \dots & \dots & \mu & 0 \end{array} \right) \tag {1.6}
$$

$$
A _ {1} ^ {(\mu)} = \left( \begin{array}{c c c c c} - (\boldsymbol {\gamma} + \lambda) & 0 & 0 & \dots & \boldsymbol {\gamma} \\ 0 & - (\boldsymbol {\mu} + \lambda) & 0 & \dots & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & - (\boldsymbol {\mu} + \lambda) & \vdots & 0 \\ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \ddots & \vdots \\ 0 & \dots & \dots & \dots - (\boldsymbol {\mu} + \lambda) \end{array} \right) \tag {1.7}
$$

Here, $\mathbf { A } _ { 3 } { = } A _ { 1 } ^ { ( \pmb { \mu } + \pmb { \mu } _ { 1 } ) }$ and $\mathbf { A } _ { 4 } { = } A _ { 2 } ^ { ( \mu + \mu _ { 1 } ) } \mathrm { ~ i . e . }$ ., matrix ${ \bf A } _ { 3 } \left( { \bf A } _ { 4 } \right)$ is obtained from ${ \bf A } _ { 1 } \left( { \bf A } _ { 2 } \right)$ by replacing μ by $( \mu _ { 2 } = \mu + \mu _ { 1 } )$ and keeping other elements as such in their respective positions. It is noticed that inter-level generator matrix $\mathbf { A } = \mathbf { A } _ { 0 } + \mathbf { A } _ { 3 } + \mathbf { A } _ { 4 }$ , which is not a function of λ.

Clearly, the QBD process Z is an extension of the standard birth-and-death process whose state space consists only of the level $\mathrm { L } _ { i }$ . When the transitions of a QBD process are independent of the level $\mathrm { L } _ { \mathrm { i } }$ , it is termed a homogeneous or level independent QBD process. The level independent QBD version has been studied extensively by several researchers and is given a very lucid treatment in the excellent text by (Latouche and Ramaswami 1999; Neuts 1981). Sivasamy (2021) investigated a state-dependent queueing model M]M1+M2/1/K-policy and obtained closed-form analytic solutions for queue length and waiting time variables. Sivasamy et al. (2019) studied the stationary characteristics of the QBD process representing the queue length of a twoserver service system. This proposed research is on an integrated queueing inventory system (IQIS) considers the same queueing model as Sivasamy (2021) but is attached to a retailer vendor (RV) supplier with an underlying two-dimensional QBD process $\mathbf { \vec { Z } } ^ { \prime }$ to represent both queue length and the inventory levels. In addition, the proposed analysis employs a similar type of method applied by Sivasamy et al. (2019) for obtaining the distribution of the Z process and other metrics of interest.

Steady State Distribution: Denote by $\boldsymbol { \Pi } = ( \boldsymbol { \pi } _ { 0 } , \boldsymbol { \pi } _ { l , } \boldsymbol { \pi } _ { 2 , } . . . )$ , the steady state distribution of Z and by $\pi _ { n }$ the row vector $\mathfrak { \pi } _ { n } = ( \pi ( \mathfrak { n } , 0 ) , . . . , \pi ( \mathfrak { n } , \mathbf { Q } ) )$ associated to level $n \in$ $\Nu _ { 0 }$ . Let $\mathbf { e } { = } \left( 1 , 1 , \ldots \right)$ be a column vector of unit elements. As the state $( 0 , \mathrm { { Q } ) }$ cannot be accessible from any other state (0, j) for ${ \bf j } = 0 , 1 , . . . , ( \mathrm { Q } { - } 1 )$ , we must set $\pi ( 0 , \mathbf { Q } )$ to a constant value zero.

We have that if the inter-level generator matrix A is irreducible, then Z is positive recurrent if and only if $\begin{array} { r } { \rho _ { 2 } = ( \alpha ^ { * } \mathbf { \Delta A _ { 0 } } \ \mathbf { e } ) / ( \alpha ^ { * } \mathbf { \Delta A _ { 4 } e } ) = \frac { \lambda ( \mu _ { 2 } + \gamma Q ) } { \mu _ { 2 } Q \gamma } \ < 1 } \end{array}$ ,

$$
\rho_ {2} = \left(\alpha * A _ {0}\right) / \left(\alpha * A _ {4}\right) = \frac {\lambda \left(\mu_ {2} + \gamma Q\right)}{\mu_ {2} Q \gamma} = \left(\frac {\lambda}{\mu_ {2}}\right) \left(\frac {1}{1 - \alpha_ {0} ^ {*}}\right) <   1 \tag {1.8}
$$

where $\mu _ { 2 } = \mu + \mu _ { 1 }$ . We now ass $\rho _ { 2 } < 1$ or $\begin{array} { r } { \frac { 1 } { Q \gamma } + \frac { 1 } { \mu _ { 2 } } < \frac { 1 } { \lambda } } \end{array}$ for the rest of the $\textsf { Z }$ policy $\cdot _ { \mathrm { f } } ,$ . We also assume that

$$
\rho_ {1} = \left(\alpha A _ {0}\right) / \left(\alpha A _ {2}\right) = \frac {\lambda \left(\mu_ {1} + \gamma Q\right)}{\mu_ {1} Q \gamma} = \left(\frac {\lambda}{\mu_ {1}}\right) \left(\frac {1}{1 - \alpha_ {0}}\right) <   1 \tag {1.9}
$$

# 1.2.1.3 Steady State Characteristics of the Inventory Cum Queueing Management

We determine the optimal policy for the inventory system with the underlying Z process that has the infinitesimal generator (1.5) subject to the stability condition attained in the above section. Berman and Kim (1999, 2001) have shown that the ordering policy $( r \geq 0 , \ Q )$ is only suboptimal. They have further proved that optimal policies play crucial role as decision variable in the queue length. Such optimal policies are of monotone structure. Let $\begin{array} { r } { \rho _ { 2 } = \left( \frac { \lambda } { \mu _ { 2 } } \right) \left( \frac { 1 } { 1 - \alpha _ { 0 } ^ { * } } \right) } \end{array}$ be denoted as ρ.

Theorem 1.1 The steady state distribution ⊓ of the process Z is given by (1.10):

$$
\begin{array}{l} \pi_ {n} = \left\{ \begin{array}{c} \Big (\frac {\beta}{1 - \alpha_ {0}} \Big) \big (\alpha_ {1}, \alpha_ {2}, \ldots , \alpha_ {Q}, 0 \big), n = 0 \\ \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {n} \alpha f o r n = 1, 2 \ldots , (K + 1) \\ \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {K + 1} \rho^ {[ n - (K + 1) ]} \alpha^ {*} f o r n = (K + 2), (K + 3), \ldots . \end{array} \right. \\ \beta = \frac {(1 - \rho) (1 - \rho_ {1})}{(1 - \rho) \left(1 - \rho_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)}\right) + (1 - \rho_ {1}) \rho_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)}} \tag {1.10} \\ \end{array}
$$

Proof Solving the matrix equations ⊓ R = 0, ⊓ e = 1 we obtain the following system of equations in terms of the sub-matrices of R and sub-vectors of ⊓:

$$
\begin{array}{l} - \pi_ {0} A _ {0} + \pi_ {1} A _ {2} = 0 \\ \pi_ {n - 1} A _ {0} + \pi_ {n} A _ {1} + \pi_ {n + 1} A _ {2} = 0 \text {f o r n = 1 , 2 , . . . K} \\ \pi_ {K} A _ {0} + \pi_ {K + 1} A _ {1} + \pi_ {K + 2} A _ {4} = 0, \quad \text {f o r n = K + 1} \\ \pi_ {n - 1} A _ {0} + \pi_ {n} A _ {3} + \pi_ {n + 1} A _ {4} = 0 \text {f o r} n = (K + 2), (K + 3), \dots \tag {1.11} \\ \end{array}
$$

Adding all equations of (1.11) for $\mathbf { n } \in \mathbf { N } _ { 0 }$ , we obtain

$$
\sum_ {n = 1} ^ {K + 1} \pi_ {n} \left(A _ {0} + A _ {1} + A _ {2} = A\right) + \sum_ {n = K + 2} ^ {\infty} \pi_ {n} \left(A _ {0} + A _ {3} + A _ {4} = A *\right) = 0
$$

This implies that $\pi _ { n } A = 0 { \mathrm { ~ f o r ~ n } } = 1 , 2 , . . . , ( \mathrm { K } + 1 ) { \mathrm { ~ a n d } } \Rightarrow \pi _ { n } A ^ { * } = 0 { \mathrm { ~ f o r ~ n } } =$ $( \mathsf { K } + 2 ) , ( \mathsf { K } + 3 )$ , ….

Thus, it is observed that the non-negative vector $\pi _ { n }$ is proportional to the vector α for ${ \mathfrak { n } } = 1 , 2 , . . . , ( \mathrm { K } + 1 )$ and that the non-negative vector $\pi _ { n }$ is proportional to the vector $\alpha ^ { * }$ for ${ \mathfrak { n } } = ( \mathbf { K } + 2 ) , ( \mathbf { K } + 3 ) , \ldots .$ . We let $\bar { \bf { \pi } } _ { n } = \beta { \pmb { \alpha } } { \pmb { R } } ^ { n }$ in (1.11) for $\boldsymbol { \mathrm { n } } = 1 , 2 , \ldots$ $( \mathsf K + 1 ) , \beta > 0$ and R is a rate matrix. This substitution indicates that

$$
\begin{array}{l} \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)} \alpha \boldsymbol {R} ^ {n - 1} \left(\boldsymbol {A} _ {0} + \boldsymbol {R} \boldsymbol {A} _ {1} + \boldsymbol {R} ^ {2} \boldsymbol {A} _ {2}\right) = \mathbf {0} \\ \Rightarrow \boldsymbol {A} _ {0} + \boldsymbol {R} \boldsymbol {A} _ {1} + \boldsymbol {R} ^ {2} \boldsymbol {A} _ {2} = \mathbf {0} \tag {1.12} \\ \end{array}
$$

Add R A2 on both sides of (1.12), we get

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathbf {A} _ {0} + \mathbf {R} [ \mathbf {A} _ {2} + (\mathbf {I} + \mathbf {R}) 2 _ {4} ] = \mathbf {R A} _ {4} \\ \Rightarrow A _ {0} + \mathbf {R} \left\{\left(\mathbf {I} - \mathbf {R}\right) ^ {- 1} \left[ (\mathbf {I} - \mathbf {R}) A _ {1} + (\mathbf {I} - \mathbf {R}) (\mathbf {I} + \mathbf {R}) A _ {2} \right] \right\} = \mathbf {R} A _ {2} \\ \Rightarrow \mathbf {A} _ {0} + \mathbf {R} \left\{\left(\mathbf {I} - \mathbf {R}\right) ^ {- 1} \left[ \left(\mathbf {A} _ {1} + \mathbf {A} _ {2}\right) - \left(\mathbf {R A} _ {1} + \mathbf {R} ^ {2} \mathbf {A} _ {2}\right) \right] \right\} = \mathbf {R A} _ {2} \\ \Rightarrow A _ {0} e + \mathbf {R} \left\{\left(\mathbf {I} - \mathbf {R}\right) ^ {- 1} \left[ \left(\mathbf {A} _ {0} + \mathbf {A} _ {1} + \mathbf {A} _ {2}\right) - \left(\mathbf {A} _ {0} + \mathbf {R} \mathbf {A} _ {1} + \mathbf {R} ^ {2} \mathbf {A} _ {2}\right) \right] \right\} e = \mathbf {R} \mathbf {A} _ {2} e \\ \Rightarrow A _ {0} e + \mathbf {R} \big \{(\mathbf {I} - \mathbf {R}) ^ {- 1} [ 0 - 0 ] \big \} e = \mathbf {R} A _ {4} e \\ \Rightarrow A _ {0} \mathbf {e} = \mathbf {R} A _ {4} \mathbf {e} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\boldsymbol {A} _ {0} \boldsymbol {e} = \mathbf {R} \boldsymbol {A} _ {2} \boldsymbol {e} \Rightarrow \alpha \boldsymbol {A} _ {0} \boldsymbol {e} = \alpha \mathbf {R} \boldsymbol {A} _ {2} \boldsymbol {e} \tag {1.13}
$$

Since $\rho _ { 1 } { = } ( \alpha \mathrm { A } _ { 0 } \ : \mathrm { e } ) / ( \alpha \mathrm { A } _ { 2 } \ : \mathrm { e } )$ of (1.9), the equality statement in (1.13) is satisfied if and only if the rate matrix R is a diagonal matrix with each diagonal element is $\rho _ { 1 }$ $\mathrm { i . e . , } \mathbf { R } = \mathrm { d i a g } ( \rho _ { 1 } )$ .

Now, we let ${ \pmb { \pi } } _ { n + K } = ( { \pmb { \pi } } _ { 1 + K } ) e \beta { \pmb { \alpha } } ^ { * } { \bf { R } } ^ { \mathrm { n - 1 } }$ in (1.11) for $\mathfrak { n } = 1 , 2 , \ldots ( \mathbf { K } + 1 ) , \beta > 0$ and R is another rate matrix. This substitution indicates that

$$
\begin{array}{l} \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)} \alpha^ {*} \mathbf {R} ^ {n - 1} \left(\mathbf {A} _ {0} + \mathbf {R} \mathbf {A} _ {3} + \mathbf {R} ^ {2} \mathbf {A} _ {4}\right) = 0 \\ \Rightarrow \boldsymbol {A} _ {0} + \mathbf {R} \boldsymbol {A} _ {3} + \mathbf {R} ^ {2} \boldsymbol {A} _ {4} = 0 \tag {1.14} \\ \end{array}
$$

As before after some manipulations we can show that

$$
\alpha^ {*} A _ {0} e = \alpha^ {*} \mathbf {R} A _ {4} e \Rightarrow \mathbf {R} = \operatorname {d i a g} (\rho_ {2}) \tag {1.15}
$$

Now substituting the value of ${ \bf { \pi } } \pi _ { 1 } = \beta { \pmb { \alpha } } { \bf { R } }$ to $- { \pmb \pi } _ { 0 } { \pmb A } _ { 0 } + { \pmb \pi } _ { 1 } { \pmb A } _ { 2 } = 0$ and simplifying we get

$$
\begin{array}{l} \pi_ {0} A _ {0} = \pi_ {1} A _ {2} \Rightarrow \pi_ {0} = \pi_ {1} A _ {2} \left(A _ {0}\right) ^ {- 1} \Rightarrow \pi_ {0} = \pi_ {1} \frac {1}{\lambda} A _ {2} \\ \Rightarrow \pi_ {0} = \beta \left(\frac {\lambda}{\mu (1 - \alpha_ {0})}\right) \frac {1}{\lambda} \alpha A _ {2} = \beta \left(\frac {\lambda}{\mu (1 - \alpha_ {0})}\right) \frac {1}{\lambda} (\mu \alpha_ {1}, \mu \alpha_ {2}, \dots , \mu \alpha_ {Q}, 0) \\ = \beta (\alpha_ {1}, \alpha_ {2}, \dots , \alpha_ {Q}, 0) / (1 - \alpha_ {0}) \\ \end{array}
$$

From the normalizing equation, we can find the value of β. Thus,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \sum_ {n = 0} ^ {K} \pi_ {n} e + \sum_ {n = K + 1} ^ {\infty} \pi_ {n} e = \pi_ {0} e + \sum_ {n = 1} ^ {K} \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {n} \alpha e \\ + \sum_ {n = (K + 1)} ^ {\infty} \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {K + 1} \rho^ {[ n - (K + 1) ]} \boldsymbol {\alpha} * \boldsymbol {e} = 1 \\ \Rightarrow \beta + \beta \left[ \frac {\rho_ {1} \left(1 - \rho_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)}\right)}{1 - \rho_ {1}} + \frac {\rho_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)}}{1 - \rho} \right] = 1 \\ \Rightarrow \beta \left[ \frac {\left(1 - \rho_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)}\right)}{1 - \rho_ {1}} + \frac {\rho_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)}}{1 - \rho}) \right] = 1 \\ \Rightarrow \beta = \frac {(1 - \rho) (1 - \rho_ {1})}{(1 - \rho) \left(1 - \rho_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)}\right) + (1 - \rho_ {1}) \rho_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)}} \\ \end{array}
$$

Hence the theorem.

Lemma 1.1 The third equation of (1.11) is also satisfied by the values of (1.10) stated in the Theorem 1.1.

Proof Substitute for $\pi _ { n } = \beta \rho _ { 1 } ^ { n } \alpha$ for n = K, and(K + 1) and $\pi _ { K + 2 } = \beta \rho _ { 1 } ^ { K + 1 } \rho \alpha ^ { * }$ in the third equation of (1.11) which is now given in (1.16).

$$
\pi_ {K} A _ {0} + \pi_ {K + 1} A _ {1} + \pi_ {K + 2} A _ {4} = 0 \tag {1.16}
$$

We see that, on using $\mathbf { A } _ { 0 } + \rho _ { 1 } \mathbf { A } _ { 1 } + \rho _ { 1 } ^ { 2 } \mathbf { A } _ { 2 } = 0$ ,

$$
\begin{array}{l} \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {K} \alpha \left(\boldsymbol {A} _ {0} + \rho_ {1} \boldsymbol {A} _ {1}\right) + \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {K + 1} \rho \alpha^ {*} \boldsymbol {A} _ {4} = 0 \\ \Rightarrow \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {K} \alpha \left(A _ {0} + \rho_ {1} A _ {1} + \rho_ {1} ^ {2} A _ {2}\right) - \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {K + 2} \alpha A _ {2} + \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {K + 1} \rho \alpha^ {*} A _ {4} = 0 \\ \Rightarrow \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {K + 1} (\rho \alpha^ {*} A _ {4} - \alpha \rho_ {1} A _ {2}) = 0 \\ \Rightarrow \left(\rho \alpha^ {*} A _ {4} - \alpha \rho_ {1} A _ {2}\right) = 0 \\ \Rightarrow \rho \left(\alpha^ {*} A e\right) = \alpha \left(\rho_ {1} A _ {2} e\right) \tag {1.17} \\ \end{array}
$$

which implies that $\rho _ { 1 } { = } ( \alpha \mathbf { A } _ { 0 } \mathbf { e } ) / ( \alpha \mathbf { A } _ { 2 } \mathbf { e } )$ and $( \rho _ { 2 } { = } ) \rho = ( \alpha ^ { * } \mathbf { A } _ { 0 } \mathbf { e } ) / ( \alpha ^ { * } \mathbf { A } _ { 4 } \mathbf { e } ) ,$ .

Therefore, all the equations given by (1.11) are now solved to have a unique solution ⊓ as stated in (1.10) of Theorem 1.1. As the result $\Pi > 0$ and $\Pi { \bf e } = 1$ it sets that the bi-variate sequence Z process is an ergodic process on the state space $\mathbf { E } _ { \mathrm { f } ^ { - } } \{ ( 0 \ Q ) \}$ . This type of stationary distribution $\Pi > 0$ and $\Pi { \bf e } = 1$ is first derived for the $\mathbf { M } / \mathbf { M } _ { 1 } + \mathbf { M } _ { 2 } / 1 / \mathbf { K } \mathbf { \mathbf { - r u l e } } / \mathbf { f } _ { 1 }$ -policy model.

# 1.3 Performance Measures for the Inventory-Queueing System

Consider the results derived from the integration of quieting inventory system. Denote the conditional average inventory levels: by I(0) for N  0, by I(1) for N  1, 2, …, K and by I(2) given that N = (K + 1), (K + 2), …. Using the results of the preceding inter-play and the stable distributions, we get, using $\Pi > 0 .$ , and $\Pi { \bf e } = 1$ :

$$
\overline {{I}} (0) = \sum_ {j = 0} ^ {\mathrm {Q - 1}} j \pi (0, j) = \beta \frac {(\mathrm {Q - 1})}{2}
$$

$$
\bar {I} (1) = \sum_ {n = 0} ^ {K} \sum_ {j = 0} ^ {Q} j \pi (n, j) = \beta (1 - \alpha_ {0}) \frac {(Q + 1)}{2} \left[ \frac {\rho_ {1} \left(1 - \rho_ {1} ^ {K}\right)}{1 - \rho_ {1}} \right]
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \bar {I} (2) = \sum_ {n = K + 1} ^ {\infty} \sum_ {j = 0} ^ {Q} j \pi (n, j) = \beta \frac {\rho_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)}}{1 - \rho} \sum_ {j = 0} ^ {Q} j \alpha_ {j} ^ {*} \\ = \beta \frac {\rho_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)}}{1 - \rho} \left(1 - \alpha_ {0} ^ {*}\right) \left(\frac {(Q + 1)}{2}\right) \tag {1.18} \\ \end{array}
$$

Thus, the unconditional average inventory is $\overline { { I } } \left( > 1 \right) = \overline { { \mathrm { I } } } ( 1 ) + \overline { { \mathrm { I } } } ( 2 )$ is given by

$$
\bar {I} (> 1) = \beta \frac {(Q + 1)}{2} \left[ (1 - \alpha_ {0}) \left(\frac {\rho_ {1} \left(1 - \rho_ {1} ^ {K}\right)}{1 - \rho_ {1}}\right) + \frac {\left(1 - \alpha_ {0} ^ {*}\right) \rho_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)}}{(1 - \rho)} \right] \tag {1.19}
$$

The marginal mean queue length in the system (queue + service) L is given by

$$
\begin{array}{l} L = \sum_ {n = 0} ^ {K} n \pi_ {n} e + \sum_ {n = K + 1} ^ {\infty} n \pi_ {n} e \\ = \beta \rho_ {1} \left[ \frac {1 - \rho_ {1} ^ {K + 1} - (K + 1) (1 - \rho_ {1}) \rho_ {1} ^ {K}}{(1 - \rho_ {1}) ^ {2}} \right] \\ + \beta \rho_ {1} \left[ \frac {\rho \rho_ {1} ^ {K} + (K + 1) (1 - \rho) \rho_ {1} ^ {K}}{(1 - \rho) ^ {2}} \right] \tag {1.20} \\ \end{array}
$$

The marginal mean queue length in the queue (queue only) $\mathrm { L _ { q } }$ is given by

$$
\mathrm {L} _ {\mathrm {q}} = \sum_ {\mathrm {n} = 1} ^ {\mathrm {K}} (\mathrm {n} - 1) \left(\pi_ {\mathrm {n}} e\right) + \sum_ {\mathrm {n} = \mathrm {K} + 1} ^ {\infty} (\mathrm {n} - 1) \pi_ {\mathrm {n}} e = \mathrm {L} - (1 - \pi_ {0} e) = \mathrm {L} - (1 - \beta) \tag {1.21}
$$

The Little’s Law: The random variable $\mathbf { \Delta ^ { 6 6 } W ^ { \prime 5 } }$ represents the total system waiting time (service queue) and $" \mathrm { W _ { q } } ^ { , , }$ represents the total waiting time of the queue (excluding service time). Then regardless of the number of servers, queuing rules or other specific cases, the conservation equation, also known as Little’s Law, is valid for almost any system of the Markovian type or not.

$$
\mathrm {L} = \lambda \mathrm {W} \text {o r} \mathrm {L} _ {q} = \lambda \mathrm {W} _ {q} \tag {1.22}
$$

Corollary 1.1 $H K < \infty ,$ , the steady state probability function $P ( N > O , I = j )$ of the M/M/1/K/ f -policy model is given by (1.23) for j 0,1,.., Q and $N = I , 2 , . . . , K .$ Let’s discuss this.

$$
\mathrm {P} (\mathrm {K} \geq \mathrm {N} > 0, \mathrm {I} = \mathrm {j}) = \beta \sum_ {(n = 1)} ^ {K} \rho_ {1} ^ {n} \alpha_ {j} = \beta \frac {\rho_ {1} \left(1 - \rho_ {1} ^ {K}\right)}{\left(1 - \rho_ {1}\right)} \alpha_ {j}
$$

$$
\mathrm {P} (\mathrm {N} \geq (\mathrm {K} + 1), \mathrm {I} = \mathrm {j}) = \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {K + 1} \sum_ {n = K = 1} ^ {\infty} \rho^ {[ n - (K + 1) ]} \alpha_ {j} ^ {*} = \beta \frac {\rho_ {1} ^ {K + 1})}{(1 - \rho)} \alpha_ {j} ^ {*}
$$

$$
P (N = 0, I = j) = \frac {\beta}{1 - \alpha_ {0}} \alpha_ {j + 1} \quad f o r j = 0, 1, \dots , (Q - 1) \tag {1.23}
$$

Corollary 1.2 The mean reorder rate (MRR) is given by.

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {M R R} = \beta \frac {\left(1 - \rho_ {1} ^ {K}\right)}{\left(1 - \rho_ {1}\right)} \left(\frac {\lambda \gamma}{\mu + Q \gamma}\right) + \beta \frac {\rho_ {1} ^ {K + 1}}{\left(1 - \rho\right)} \left(\frac {\lambda \gamma}{\mu + \mu_ {1} + Q \gamma}\right) \\ \rightarrow \frac {\lambda \gamma}{\lambda + Q \gamma} \text {a s} K \rightarrow \infty , \rho = \rho_ {1} \tag {1.24} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
w h e r e \beta \rightarrow (1 - \rho) a s K \rightarrow \infty
$$

$$
\mathrm {M R R} \rightarrow \frac {\lambda}{Q} \text {a s} \mathrm {K} \rightarrow \infty \text {a n d} \gamma \rightarrow \infty
$$

Proof Since the reorder is triggered for the $\mathbf { Z }$ process under the K-rule and f-policy just after reaching the state $( \mathbf { n } > 0 , 0 )$ by a service completion from the state $( { \mathfrak { n } } +$ $^ { 1 , 1 ) }$ at rate $\mu$ for $\boldsymbol { \mathrm { n } } = 1 , 2 , . . . , \boldsymbol { \mathrm { K } }$ and for ${ \mathfrak { n } } = ( { \mathbf { K } } + 1 ) , ( { \mathbf { K } } + 2 )$ , …, at the rate $( \mu +$ $\mu _ { 1 } )$ , MRR is computed for the Z process is computed by:

$$
\mathrm {M R R} = \sum_ {n = 1} ^ {K} \mu \pi (n + 1, 1) + \sum_ {n = K + 1} ^ {\infty} (\mu + \mu_ {1}) \pi (n + 1, 1) \tag {1.25}
$$

Substituting for $\pi ( n , 1 )$ in (1.25), we can compute the results of (1.24).

Operating Costs: There are costs associated with operating the system, arising both from customer queuing and from goods held in the system. We have a fixed holding cost $\mathbf { \dot { h } } ^ { \prime }$ per item per unit time, a fixed inventory cost $\cdot _ { \mathrm { g } } ,$ per order, and a cost $\mathbf { \Delta } ^ { 6 } \mathbf { V } \mathbf { W } ^ { 3 }$ per customer per unit waiting time. Revenue R is paid to the system each time customer service is completed. There is a policy in place to determine how much stock to order at each decision time. These decisive epochs are arrival and departure times, as well as order arrival times (Schwarz and Daduna 2006).

We always assume that there is at most one outstanding order. With these costs, the total expected cost (TEC) is:

$$
\mathrm {T E C} = \bar {I} h + M R R g + \bar {W} _ {\mathrm {q}} \mathrm {V} _ {\mathrm {w}} \tag {1.26}
$$

Remark If $\nu = \infty$ the inventory on hand leaves state (1,0) immediately to the state $( 1 , \mathrm { { Q } ) }$ , so the state space is then given by $E _ { f } = \{ ( n , j ) \colon n \in \{ 0 , 1 , \ldots , \} , 1 \leq j \leq 0 \}$ . If in addition the order size $\mathbf { Q } < \infty$ is fixed then the inventory positions are uniformly distributed, $i . e . , \mathrm { P ( I = j ) } = 1 / \mathrm { Q } \mathrm { f o r j } = 1 , 2 , . . . , \mathrm { Q }$ .

Economic Order quantity: We now illustrate a numerical exercise by fixing the inventory holding cost h $125.25 per unit, $\mathbf { g } = \$ 700.2$ and $\mathrm { V _ { w } } = \$ 380.2$ and the other input values are randomly selected as $( \lambda = 3 . 7 5 , \mu = 5 . 0 , \mu _ { 1 } = 0 . 5 , \gamma =$ 4.2, K  4) such that these values satisfy the steady state condition of the Z-process under study for $\mathrm { Q } \in \{ 4 , 5 , 6 , 7 , 8 , 9 , 1 0 , 1 1 , 1 2 \}$ . It can be shown that the optimum order quantity $\mathbf { Q } ^ { * }$ that minimizes the TEC is given by

$$
Q ^ {*} = \sqrt {\frac {2 \lambda g}{h}} = \sqrt {\frac {(2) (3 . 7 5) (7 0 0 . 2)}{1 2 5 . 2 5}} = \sqrt {4 1 . 8 4} \simeq 6 o r 7 n e a r e s t i n t e g e r \tag {1.27}
$$

The corresponding minimum cost is

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {T E C} 1 = \bar {I}, \mathrm {T E C} 2 = \mathrm {g}, \mathrm {T E C} 3 = \bar {\mathrm {W}} _ {\mathrm {q}} \mathrm {V} _ {\mathrm {w}} \\ \mathrm {T E C} = \min  (\mathrm {T E C} 1 + \mathrm {T E C} 2 + \mathrm {T E C} 3) \tag {1.28} \\ \end{array}
$$

The corresponding values of the TEC are computed and the numerical results of TEC1, TEC2 and TEC3 are drawn in a single Fig. 1.1.

Figure 1.1 shows the relationship between the various order quantity values considered from $\mathrm { Q } = 4 \mathrm { t o } \mathrm { Q } = 1 2$ and the different costs:

TEC1 = Holding cost which, generally, increases with increasing Q values.

TEC2 = Ordering cost, generally, decreases with increasing values of Q.

TEC3 = Waiting time cost, generally, decreases with increasing values of Q.

Further, it is noticed that the costs of TEC1 and TEC2 are almost equal at the position $( \mathrm { Q } = 6 , \mathrm { T E C } 2 = 3 6 2 . 4 4 = \mathrm { T E C } 1 )$ . Similarly, the costs of TEC1 and TEC3 are almost equal at the position $( \mathrm { Q } = 7 , \mathrm { T E C } 3 = 4 1 7 . 7 8 = \mathrm { T E C } 1 )$ . The SCM must know these costs for comparing with the optimum order quantity and the optimum TEC.

![](images/0b87bcf1a877553eab9910700c6b0d146904c0ea3f9294eeb59f8c947ae83529.jpg)

![](images/609ccddcc1b4f51ddbf8dec6a8dfc41d78307ac152c9c8e002f8f8ba90eaaa5c.jpg)  
Fig. 1.1 Graph of Q versus TEC for h = $125.25 per unit, $\mathrm { g } = \mathbb { S } 7 0 0 . 2$ and $\mathrm { V _ { w } } = { \mathbb { S } } 3 8 0 . 2 \ \mathrm { a n d } , ( \lambda$ $= 3 . 7 5 , \mu = 5 . 0 , \mu _ { 1 } = 0 . 5 , \gamma = 4 . 2 , \mathrm { K } = 4 ) \colon \mathrm { Q } = 4 , 5 , 6 , 7 , 8 , 9 , 1 0 , 1 1 , 1 2$

From the Fig. 1.1, it is found the minimum total expected cost is $1137.66 which occurs against the optimum order quantity is 8, i.e.,

$$
\operatorname {T E C} (Q * = 8) = \$ 1 1 3 7. 6 6.
$$

Thus, the optimum $\mathrm { T E C } ( \mathrm { Q ^ { * } } = 8 ) = \mathbb { { \mathbb { { S } } } } 1 1 3 7 . 6 6$ does not match with either the cost $\mathrm { ( Q = 6 , T E C 2 = 3 6 2 . 4 4 = T E C 1 ) ~ o r ~ o f ~ ( Q = 7 , T E C 3 = 4 1 7 . 7 8 = T E C 1 ) }$ .

# 1.4 Results for the SCM Attached to M/M/1 Queues with Zero Lead Time

Let us consider the case when $\gamma \to \infty$ i.e., the replenishment lead time vanishes. This indicates that the order we place for Q units when the Z-process reaches the state $( \mathrm { n } 2 , 1 , 0 )$ immediately the ordered quantity $\mathrm { \bf Q } \left( \mathrm { > } 0 \right)$ is delivered instantaneously; this event forces the next state of the Z-process to move to the state $( 1 , \mathrm { { Q } } )$ . Thus, the system cannot visit the state (0, 0) of the Z-process where $\alpha _ { 0 } = 0$ and li m $\begin{array} { r } { ( \alpha _ { j } = \frac { \gamma } { \mu + Q \gamma } ) = \frac { 1 } { Q } } \end{array}$ γ→∞ for ${ \mathrm { j } } = 1 , 2 , . . , 0$ . This applies also for $\alpha ^ { * } { } _ { 0 } = 0$ and $\begin{array} { r } { l i m \left( \alpha _ { j } ^ { * } = \frac { \gamma } { \mu + \mu _ { 1 } + Q \gamma } \right) = \frac { 1 } { Q } } \end{array}$ for j $= 1 , 2 , . . , Q .$ .

• If $\mathrm Q = 1$ , and $\gamma \to \infty$ then from the results of Theorem 1.1, we observe that the steady state characteristics of the model $\mathbf { M } / \mathbf { M } _ { 1 } + \mathbf { M } _ { 2 } / 1 / \mathbf { K } .$ -rule is ensured if $\rho =$ $\lambda / ( \mu + \mu _ { 1 } ) < 1$ , irrespective of the value of $\rho _ { 1 } = \lambda / \mu < 1$ or not.   
• If $ Q > 1 , \gamma < \infty$ and either $\kappa  \infty , \mathrm { o r } \mu _ { 1 } = 0$ , then the K-policy rule cannot be implemented.

So, one can extract the results of M/M/1 queueing system attached to the inventory system under $\operatorname { f } _ { 1 } - \operatorname { p o l i c y }$ from the result of Theorem 1.1 by letting either $\kappa  \ \infty , \mathrm { o r } \ \mu _ { 1 } \ = 0$ . Thus, expressions for the stationary probabilities $\pi _ { n } , \mathrm { ~ n ~ } =$ $0 , 1 , 2 , . . . , \infty .$ , and $\alpha _ { j } , \mathrm { ~ j = 0 } , 1 , 2 , . . . , \mathrm { Q }$ are

$$
\begin{array}{l} \pi_ {n} = \beta \rho_ {1} ^ {n} \alpha , \alpha = (\alpha_ {0}, \alpha_ {1}, \alpha_ {2}, \dots , \alpha_ {Q}), \alpha_ {0} = \frac {\mu}{Q \gamma + \mu}, \alpha_ {j} = \frac {\gamma}{Q \gamma + \mu} \\ \text {w h e r e} \beta = 1 - \left(\frac {\lambda}{\mu \left(1 - \alpha_ {0}\right)}\right), \rho = \rho_ {1} = \left(\frac {\lambda}{\mu \left(1 - \alpha_ {0}\right)}\right) \\ \text {a n d} \pi_ {0} = \frac {\beta}{1 - \alpha_ {0}} \left(\alpha_ {1}, \alpha_ {2}, \dots , \alpha_ {Q}, 0\right), \sum_ {n = 0} ^ {\infty} \pi_ {n} e = 1 \tag {1.29} \\ \end{array}
$$

Corollary 1.3 As $K \to \infty$ , the steady state probability function $P ( N > O , I = j )$ of the M/M/1 queue under f-policy is given by, for j = 0, 1,.., Q

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {P} (\mathrm {N} > 0, \mathrm {I} = 0) = \frac {\lambda}{\lambda + \mathrm {Q} \gamma} \text {a n d} \mathrm {P} (\mathrm {N} > 0, \mathrm {I} = \mathrm {j}) = \frac {\rho \gamma}{\lambda + \mathrm {Q} \gamma} \\ P (N = 0, I = j) = \frac {\beta}{1 - \alpha_ {0}} \frac {\gamma}{\mu + Q \gamma}, \text {f o r} j = 0, 1, 2, \dots , (Q - 1) \tag {1.30} \\ \end{array}
$$

Proof Application of the limiting condition $\mathrm { K } \to \infty$ in the results of Theorem 1.1 leads to.

$$
\begin{array}{c} \mathrm {P (N > 0 , I = 0) = \sum_ {n = 1} ^ {\infty} \pi_ {n} e \alpha_ {0} = \beta \sum_ {n = 1} ^ {\infty} \rho_ {1} ^ {n} \alpha_ {0} = (1 - \rho) \Big (\frac {\rho}{1 - \rho} \Big) \frac {\mu}{(\mu + Q \gamma)} = \frac {\lambda}{\lambda + Q \gamma},} \\ \text {s i n c e .} \end{array}
$$

$$
\beta = 1 - \rho , \alpha_ {0} = \frac {\mu}{Q \gamma + \mu}, \alpha_ {j} = \frac {\gamma}{Q \gamma + \mu}.
$$

$$
\text {F u r t h e r , f o r j = 1 , \overline {{2}} ; \dots , Q}
$$

$$
\mathrm {P} (\mathrm {N} > 0, \mathrm {I} = \mathrm {j}) = \sum_ {n = 1} ^ {\infty} \pi_ {n} e \alpha_ {j} = \beta \sum_ {n = 1} ^ {\infty} \rho_ {1} ^ {n} \alpha_ {j} = (1 - \rho) \left(\frac {\rho}{1 - \rho}\right) \frac {\gamma}{(\mu + Q \gamma)} = \frac {\rho \gamma}{\lambda + Q \gamma},
$$

$$
\mathrm {P} (\mathrm {N} = 0, \mathrm {I} = \mathrm {j}) = \frac {\beta}{1 - \alpha_ {0}} \frac {\gamma}{\mu + Q \gamma} = \frac {1 - \rho}{1 - \alpha_ {0}} \frac {\gamma}{\mu + Q \gamma}, \quad \text {f o r j = 0 , 1 , 2 , . . . , (Q - 1)}
$$

It is remarked that when $\mu _ { 1 } = 0$ , we see that $\rho _ { 1 } = \rho$ and $\alpha _ { 0 } = \alpha _ { _ { 0 } } ^ { \ast }$ . Thus, we can obtain from the results of M/M/1/K-rule queue to that of the M/M/1 queue attached to the inventory system under $\operatorname { f } _ { 1 } - \operatorname { p o l i c y }$ discussed by (Schwarz and Daduna 2006):

$$
\begin{array}{l} \bar {I} (> 1) = \beta (1 - \alpha_ {0}) \left(\frac {\rho}{1 - \rho}\right) \frac {(Q + 1)}{2}, \beta = 1 - \rho \\ \bar {I} = \beta \left[ \frac {(Q - 1)}{2} + (1 - \alpha_ {0}) \frac {(Q + 1)}{2} \left(\frac {\rho}{1 - \rho}\right) \right] \tag {1.31} \\ \end{array}
$$

Recall that, as $\gamma \to \infty$ , we have $\begin{array} { r } { l i m \left( \alpha _ { 0 } = \frac { \mu } { \mu + Q \nu } \right) = 0 } \end{array}$ and $\beta = ( 1 - \rho )$ . Thus

$$
\begin{array}{l} \lim  _ {\gamma \rightarrow \infty} \bar {I} (> 1) = \rho \frac {(Q + 1)}{2} \text {a n d} \lim  _ {\gamma \rightarrow \infty} \bar {I} = (1 - \rho) \frac {(Q - 1)}{2} + \rho \frac {(Q + 1)}{2} \\ = \frac {(Q - 1)}{2} + \rho \\ \end{array}
$$

From (1.31) we see that li m $\overline { { I } } ( > 1 ) = \rho = l i m \overline { { I } } ,$ , if $\mathrm Q = 1$ as expected. γ→∞

Corollary 1.4 As $K  \infty ,$ , under the steady state conditions, the mean waiting time.

$\overline { { W } } _ { q } f o r$ customers of the M/M/1 queue under f-policy is given by

$$
\bar {W} _ {q} = \frac {\mathrm {L} - \rho}{\lambda} = \frac {\rho}{\mu (1 - \rho)}, \mathrm {L} = \frac {\beta \rho}{(1 - \rho) ^ {2}} = \frac {\rho}{(1 - \rho)} \tag {1.32}
$$

We define the asymptotic total cost function as in Berman and Kim (1999, 2001) for the case of M/M/1 queue with attached inventory under $\mathrm { f + \mathrm { - p o l i c y } }$ :

$$
\mathrm {T E C} = \bar {I} \mathrm {h} + \mathrm {M R R A} + \bar {W} _ {q} v _ {w} = [ (Q - 1) / 2 + \rho ] h + (\lambda / Q) A + [ \rho / \mu (1 - \rho) ] V _ {w}
$$

The optimal order quantity is the classical economic order quantity.

$$
Q * = \sqrt {\frac {2 \lambda A}{h}}
$$

Total $\mathrm { p r o f i t } = \lambda { \bf R } - \mathrm { T E C } .$

# 1.5 SCM Attached $\mathbf { M } / \mathbf { M } _ { 1 } + \mathbf { M } _ { 2 } / \mathbf { 1 } / \mathbf { K } \mathbf { - r u l e } / \mathbf { f } _ { 2 } \mathbf { - P o l i c y }$ with Dissatisfied Customers

This section deals with the same $\mathbf { M } / \mathbf { M } _ { 1 } + \mathbf { M } _ { 2 } / 1 / \mathbf { K } \mathbf { - r u l e } / \mathbf { f } _ { 2 } \mathbf { - p o l i c y }$ queueing-inventory system under a new $\operatorname { f } _ { 2 } - \operatorname { p o l i c y }$ . This $\mathbf { f } _ { 2 } { \mathrm { - p o l i c y } }$ retains all norms of the threshold version of the $\operatorname { f } _ { 1 } { \mathrm { - p o l i c y } }$ with an additional constraint over the states of the stationary ${ \bf Z } -$ process.

# f 2-policy

(i) $\mathbf { f } _ { 2 ^ { - } }$ policy retains all standards from the threshold version of $\operatorname { f } _ { 1 } - \operatorname { p o l i c y }$   
(ii) Customers are served independently of the warehouse inventory process. It ensures that the Stationary queue length distributions $\{ \mathrm { P } ( \mathbf { N } = \mathrm { n } ) = \pi _ { n } \colon \mathrm { n } = 0 .$ , $2 , \ldots \}$ and the inventory level distribution $\{ \mathrm { P } ( \mathbf { I } = \mathbf { j } ) = \alpha _ { j } \colon \mathbf { j } = 0 , 1 , 2 , \ldots \mathrm { Q } \}$ are independent probability distributions.

We now address some of the expected issues by the action of the additional norm (ii) of the new $\mathbf { f } _ { 2 } { \mathrm { - p o l i c y } }$ . If the state of process Z changes to $( \mathbf { N } = \mathrm { n } , \mathbf { I } = \mathrm { j } )$ at the time the service ends the next service will start immediately for every $( \mathbf { n } \mathbf { \Gamma } \mathbf { j } ) \in \mathbf { E } _ { \mathbf { f } } $ . Also, at the service completion point if the server moves to process state $( \mathbf { N } = \mathrm { n } > 1$ , I $= 0 )$ not only is the order placed immediately but the front-line client also receives

service in the meantime (i.e., the time it’s out of stock) and leaving the system with no product for which the customers have been queued for some time before their service begins. We call this category of customers who leave the system without a unit of the inventory as dissatisfied customers.

Let $\begin{array} { r } { \rho _ { 1 } = \frac { \lambda } { \mu _ { 1 } } } \end{array}$ and $\begin{array} { r } { \rho = \frac { \lambda } { \mu + \mu _ { 1 } } } \end{array}$ . We consider all these variations over the level 1 independent sub-matrices of the infinitesimal generator R of the Z-process:

$$
\boldsymbol {A} _ {0} = \left( \begin{array}{c c c c c} \lambda & 0 & \dots & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & \lambda & \dots & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & \ddots & \vdots & 0 \\ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \ddots & \vdots \\ 0 & \dots & \dots & 0 & \lambda \end{array} \right), \boldsymbol {A} _ {2} ^ {(\mu)} = \left( \begin{array}{c c c c c} \mu & 0 & \dots & 0 & 0 \\ \mu & 0 & \dots & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & \mu & 0 & \vdots & 0 \\ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \ddots & \vdots \\ 0 & \dots & \dots & \mu & 0 \end{array} \right)
$$

$$
A _ {1} ^ {(\mu)} = \left( \begin{array}{c c c c c} - (\boldsymbol {\gamma} + \boldsymbol {\lambda} + \boldsymbol {\mu}) & 0 & 0 & \dots & \boldsymbol {\gamma} \\ 0 & - (\boldsymbol {\mu} + \boldsymbol {\lambda}) & 0 & \dots & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & - (\boldsymbol {\mu} + \boldsymbol {\lambda}) & \vdots & 0 \\ \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \ddots & \vdots \\ 0 & \dots & \dots & \dots - (\boldsymbol {\mu} + \boldsymbol {\lambda}) \end{array} \right),
$$

and

$$
\mathbf {R} = \begin{array}{c} L _ {0} L _ {1} L _ {2} \dots L _ {K - 1} L _ {K} L _ {K + 1} L _ {K + 2} L _ {K + 3} \dots \\ \left( \begin{array}{c c c c c c c c c c} (A _ {1} + A _ {2}) & A _ {0} & 0 & \dots & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & \dots \\ A _ {2} & A _ {1} & A _ {0} & \ddots & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & \dots \\ 0 & A _ {2} & A _ {1} & \ddots & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & \dots \\ \vdots & \ddots & \ddots & \ddots & \ddots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \vdots \\ L _ {K - 1} & 0 & 0 & 0 & \ddots & A _ {1} & A _ {0} & 0 & 0 & \dots \\ L _ {K} & 0 & 0 & 0 & \ddots & A _ {2} & A _ {1} & A _ {0} & 0 & \ddots \\ 0 & 0 & 0 & \dots & 0 & A _ {2} & A _ {1} & A _ {0} & 0 & \ddots \\ 0 & 0 & 0 & \dots & 0 & 0 & A _ {4} & A _ {3} & A _ {0} & \ddots \\ \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \ddots & 0 & 0 & 0 & A _ {4} & A _ {3} & \ddots \\ \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & A _ {4} & \ddots \\ \vdots & \vdots & \vdots & \ddots & \dots & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & \ddots . \end{array} \right) \end{array}
$$

This means that we now deal with the same $\mathbf { M } / \mathbf { M } _ { 1 } + \mathbf { M } _ { 2 } / 1 / \mathbf { K } \mathbf { - r u l }$ e based queueinginventory system under the new $\operatorname { f } _ { 2 } - \operatorname { p o l i c y } .$ . We have same the inter-level generator matrix A as before. Thus,

$$
\alpha_ {0} = \frac {\mu}{\mu + Q \gamma}, \text {a n d} \alpha_ {j} \frac {\gamma}{\mu + Q \gamma} \text {f o r} j = 1, \dots , Q
$$

$$
\alpha_ {0} ^ {*} = \frac {\mu + \mu_ {1}}{\mu + \mu_ {1} + Q \gamma}, \text {a n d} \alpha_ {j} ^ {*} = \frac {\gamma}{\mu + \mu_ {1} + Q \gamma} \text {f o r} j = 1, \dots , Q
$$

So, the Z process is positive recurrent under the new threshold type $\mathbf { f } _ { 2 } { \mathrm { - p o l i c y } }$ if and only if

$$
\theta = \left(\alpha * A _ {0} e\right) / \left(\alpha * A _ {4} e\right) = \frac {\lambda}{\mu_ {2}} <   1, \theta <   1 \Rightarrow \lambda <   \mu_ {2} \tag {1.33}
$$

where $\mu _ { 2 } = \mu + \mu _ { 1 }$ . We also assume $\begin{array} { r } { \theta _ { 1 } = \frac { \lambda } { \mu _ { 1 } } < 1 } \end{array}$ for further analysis.

Theorem 1.2 The steady state distribution ⊓ of Z (now the new under $\cdot f _ { 2 } { - p o l i c y ) }$ is given.

$$
\boldsymbol {\pi} _ {n} = \left\{ \begin{array}{c} \beta \theta_ {1} ^ {n} \boldsymbol {\alpha}, \boldsymbol {\alpha} = \left(\alpha_ {0}, \alpha_ {1}, \alpha_ {2}, \ldots , \alpha_ {Q}\right) \text {f o r} n = 0, 1, 2, \ldots , K \\ \beta \theta_ {1} ^ {K + 1} \theta^ {[ n - (K + 1) ]} \boldsymbol {\alpha} *, \boldsymbol {\alpha} * = \left(\alpha_ {0} ^ {*}, \alpha_ {0} ^ {*}, \alpha_ {0} ^ {*}, \ldots , \alpha_ {Q} ^ {*}\right) \text {f o r} n = (K + 1), (K + 2), \ldots , \end{array} \right.
$$

$$
\beta = \frac {(1 - \theta) (1 - \theta_ {1})}{(1 - \theta) \left(1 - \theta_ {1} ^ {(\mathrm {K} + 1)}\right) + (1 - \theta_ {1}) \theta_ {1} ^ {(\mathrm {K} + 1)}} \tag {1.34}
$$

Proof Solving $\pmb { \Pi } \mathbf { R } = 0$ , we obtain that.

$$
\pi_ {0} (\mathbf {A} _ {1} + \mathbf {A} _ {2}) + \pi_ {1} \mathbf {A} _ {2} = 0
$$

$$
\pi_ {n - 1} \mathbf {A} _ {0} + \pi_ {n} \mathbf {A} _ {1} + \pi_ {n + 1} \mathbf {A} _ {2} = 0 \text {f o r n = 1 , 2 , . . . K}
$$

$$
\pi_ {K} \mathbf {A} _ {0} + \pi_ {K + 1} \mathbf {A} _ {1} + \pi_ {K + 2} \mathbf {A} _ {4} = 0, \text {f o r n = K + 1}
$$

$$
\pi_ {n - 1} \mathbf {A} _ {0} + \pi_ {n} \mathbf {A} _ {3} + \pi_ {n + 1} \mathbf {A} _ {4} = 0 \text {f o r} n = (K + 2), (K + 3), \dots \tag {1.35}
$$

Adding all equations of (1.35) for $\mathbf { n } \in \mathbf { N } _ { 0 }$ , we obtain

$$
\sum_ {n = 0} ^ {K + 1} \pi_ {n} \left(A _ {0} + A _ {1} + A _ {2} = A\right) + \sum_ {n = K + 2} ^ {\infty} \pi_ {n} \left(A _ {0} + A _ {3} + A _ {4} = A *\right) = 0
$$

This implies that $\pmb { \pi } _ { n } \pmb { A } = 0$ for n = 0, 1, 2, …, (K + 1) and $\pmb { \pi } _ { n } \pmb { A } ^ { * } = 0$ for n = (K $+ 2 ) , ( \mathrm { K } + 3 ) , \ldots$ .

Thus, it is observed that the non-negative vector $\pi _ { n }$ is proportional to the vector α for $\mathbf { n } { = } 0 , 1 , 2 , . . . , ( \mathbf { K } + 1 )$ and that the non-negative vector $\pi _ { n }$ is proportional to the vector $\alpha ^ { * }$ for ${ \mathfrak { n } } = ( \mathrm { K } + 2 ) , ( \mathrm { K } + ~ 3 ) , \dots { \mathfrak { L } }$ As before, we let $\bar { \mathbf { x } } _ { n } = \beta \pmb { \alpha } \theta _ { 1 } ^ { n }$ for $\mathbf { n } =$ $0 , 1 , 2 , \ldots ( \mathbf { K } + 1 ) , \pi _ { n } = \beta \alpha ^ { * } \pmb { \theta } ^ { n } \beta > 0$ . From the normalizing equation, we can find the value of β. Thus, the results of (1.34) can be proved.

The marginal mean queue length in the system L and (queue only) $\mathrm { L _ { q } }$ are given by

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {L} = \left(L _ {1} = \sum_ {n = 0} ^ {K} \mathrm {n} \pi_ {n} e\right) + \left(L _ {2} = \sum_ {n = K + 1} ^ {\infty} \mathrm {n} \pi_ {n} e\right) \\ = \beta \theta_ {1} \left[ \frac {1 - \theta_ {1} ^ {K + 1} - (K + 1) (1 - \theta_ {1}) \theta_ {1} ^ {K}}{(1 - \theta_ {1}) ^ {2}} \right] \\ + \beta \theta_ {1} \left[ \frac {\theta \theta_ {1} ^ {K} + (K + 1) (1 - \theta) \theta_ {1} ^ {K}}{(1 - \theta) ^ {2}} \right] \tag {1.36} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
L _ {q} = \sum_ {n = 1} ^ {K} (\mathrm {n} - 1) \pi_ {n} e + \sum_ {n = K + 1} ^ {\infty} (\mathrm {n} - 1) \pi_ {n} e = \mathrm {L} - (1 - \beta)
$$

The waiting time in the queue (excluding the service time) $\mathrm { W _ { q } }$ is given by $\operatorname { L } _ { q } =$ $\lambda \mathbf { W } _ { q }$ .

Corollary 1.5 The mean reorder rate (MRR) is given by.

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {M R R} = \beta \left(\frac {\lambda \mu}{\mu + Q \gamma}\right) + \beta \frac {\left(1 - \theta_ {1} ^ {K}\right)}{\left(1 - \theta_ {1}\right)} \left(\frac {\lambda \gamma}{\mu + Q \gamma}\right) \\ + \beta \frac {\theta_ {1} ^ {K + 1}}{(1 - \theta)} \left(\frac {\lambda \gamma}{\mu + \mu_ {1} + Q \gamma}\right) \tag {1.37} \\ \end{array}
$$

Proof Since the reorder is triggered for the Z-process under the K-rule and f2-policy just after reaching the state $( \mathbf { n } > 0 , 0 )$ by a service completion from the state (n + 1,1) at rate $\mu$ for $\boldsymbol { \mathrm { n } } = 1 , 2 , . . . , \boldsymbol { \mathrm { K } }$ and at rate $( \mu + \mu _ { 1 } )$ , for ${ \mathfrak { n } } = ( { \mathbf { K } } + 1 ) , ( { \mathbf { K } } + 2 )$ , … , and the state 1.0) can be reached at rate $\lambda$ , the MRR is computed for the Z process as follows:

$$
\mathrm {M R R} = \lambda \pi (0, 0) + \sum_ {n = 1} ^ {K} \mu \pi (n + 1, 1) + \sum_ {n = K + 1} ^ {\infty} (\mu + \mu_ {1}) \pi (n + 1, 1)
$$

which leads to the stated result of (1.37).

Let D  Expected value of the dissatisfied number of customers. Then, using the indicator function $P ( I = 0 / n > 0 ) = 1$ and $P ( I = j / n > 0 ) = 0 \mathrm { f o r j } = 1 , 2 , . . ,$ $\mathrm { Q } ,$ , we find that

$$
\begin{array}{l} D = \mu P (I = 0 / n > 0) = \mu \left[ \sum_ {n = 1} ^ {\infty} \pi (n, 0) \right] \\ = \mu \left[ \beta \alpha_ {0} \frac {\left(1 - \theta_ {1} ^ {K + 1}\right)}{1 - \theta_ {1}} + \beta \alpha_ {0} ^ {*} \frac {\theta_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)}}{1 - \theta} \right] \tag {1.38} \\ \end{array}
$$

Lemma 1.2 The average on hand inventory, say I, is given by.

$$
\bar {I} = \beta \alpha_ {1} \frac {\left(1 - \theta_ {1} ^ {K + 1}\right)}{1 - \theta_ {1}} + \beta \alpha_ {1} ^ {*} \frac {\theta_ {1} ^ {(K + 1)}}{1 - \theta} \tag {1.39}
$$

Proof The average on hand inventory is computed by.

$$
\bar {\mathbf {I}} = \sum_ {n = 0} ^ {K} \sum_ {j = 1} ^ {Q} j \pi (n, j) + \sum_ {n = K + 1} ^ {\infty} \sum_ {j = 1} ^ {Q} j \pi (n, j)
$$

Substitution for $\pi ( { \mathrm { n } } , \mathbf { j } ) , ( { \mathrm { n } } , \mathbf { j } ) { \in } \mathbf { E } _ { \mathrm { f } }$ will lead to (1.39).

# Total Expected Cost (TEC):

Let, h  fixed holding cost per item and time unit,

u = Fixed cost for each replenishment order.

$\mathbf { V _ { W } }$ Waiting cost per customer and time unit.

g  cost of lost sales per item and time unit,

With these costs, the total expected cost (TEC) is given

$$
\mathrm {T E C} = \bar {I} \mathrm {h} + \mathrm {M R R} \mathrm {u} + \bar {W} _ {q} v _ {w} + g D \tag {1.40}
$$

# 1.5.1 Optimum Order Quantity $^ { \dag } Q ^ { \ast , }$

Let us find an optimum order quantity, say $\boldsymbol { \mathrm { Q } } ^ { * }$ , which minimizes the TEC of (1.40). Assume that the order quantity Q increases over a period from $\mathrm { Q } = 4 \mathrm { t o } \mathrm { Q } = 1 2$ . Let the input values of queueing-inventory system be $\lambda = 3 . 7 5 , \mu = 5 . 0 , \mu _ { 1 } = 0 . 5 , \gamma =$ 4.2 and the costs of the inventory system be $\mathrm { h } = 6 2 5 . 2 5 , \mathrm { u } = 7 0 0 . 2 , \mathrm { V w } = 3 8 0 . 2 \ \mathrm { a n d }$ $\mathbf { g } = 2 9 5 . 0 \AA$ .

As the Q increases from 4 to 12, we expect that.

TEC1 I h increases, TEC2 MRR u decreases,

$\mathrm { T E C } 3 = \overline { { W } } _ { q } \nu _ { w }$ neither increases nor decreases and $\mathrm { T E C } 4 = g D$ decreases.

Figure 1.2 shows the decreasing or increasing properties between Q values and the total expected costs. In Fig. 1.2a, all four components of the TEC of (1.40) are represented while Q varies from 4 to 12. Inspecting Fig. 1.2a, we can suggest a local optimum value for Q.

(i) between TEC1 (Inventory holding cost) and TEC2 (Ordering cost) as ${ \bf Q } ^ { * } = 9$ cYanmaaGow   
(ii) between TEC1 (Inventory holding cost) and TEC4 (dissatisfaction cost) as $\mathbf { Q } ^ { * }$ $= 5$   
(iii) between TEC3 (waiting cost) and TEC4 (Ordering cost) as ${ \bf Q } ^ { * } = 9$

Inspecting Fig. 1.2b which shows relationship between the sums of (TEC1 + TEC3) and $( \mathrm { T E C } 2 + \mathrm { T E C } 4 )$ as Q increases. Inspecting Fig. 1.2b, we can trace a local optimum value for Q as ${ \bf Q } ^ { * } = 9$ .

![](images/3292fde49b88a26ffd784f2dcec0b8029008b6c3d7fef68b2dc8318a6d297ec6.jpg)

![](images/ebb44d47673d5bceda87b2ddd457d581526e5d343d72b53f3107a1591478ceb3.jpg)  
Fig. 1.2 Graph of Q versus TEC for $\lambda = 3 . 7 5 , \mu = 5 . 0 , \mu _ { 1 } = 0 . 5 , \gamma = 4 . 2 , \mathrm { K } = 4 \mathrm { a n d h } = 6 2 5 . 2 5 ,$ , u = 700.2, Vw = 380.2 and g = 295.0. a (TEC1, TEC3) vs (TEC2, TEC4) for M/M/1/K-rule/f-policy. b (TEC1 + TEC3) vs (TEC2 + TEC4) for M/M/1/K-rule/f-policy

Here the term ‘optimum’ value means the $\mathbf { \bar { Q } } ^ { \prime }$ value in Fig. 1.2 against which the values of $( \mathrm { T E C 1 } + \mathrm { T E C } 2 )$ and $( \mathrm { T E C } 3 + \mathrm { T E C } 4 )$ are almost equal. Similar type of an optimum is suggested by inspecting the Fig. 1.2a also.

# 1.6 Conclusion

This paper extended the investigations of queueing-inventory management due to (Schwarz, M, and Daduna, H, 2006), replacing the M/M/1/∞/FCFS facility by an $\mathbf { M } / \mathbf { M } _ { 1 } + \mathbf { M } _ { 2 } / 1 / \mathbf { K } \mathbf { \mathbf { \bar { \tau } } } \mathbf { r u l e } / \mathbf { F C F S }$ facility. A typical type of supply chain (SC) model was discussed as an integrated queueing-inventory system (IQIS). It has a distribution centre (DC) for delivering the orders to a retailer vendor (RV) that runs an $\mathbf { M } / \mathbf { M } _ { 1 } +$ $\mathbf { M } _ { 2 } / 1 / \mathrm { K } \mathrm { - r u l e }$ based service unit. The ordering policy is of threshold type i.e., (queue length, Inventory $\mathrm { l e v e l } ) = ( \mathrm { n } > 0 , 0 )$ for placing an order of Q units. This policy monitors the periodical changes of queue length of demands and the inventory status and thus fixing the reorder instant $( \mathbf { n } > 0 , 0 )$ as an ideal point during stock out periods. The three sequences of replenishment periods, inter-arrival times and service times are assumed to be mutually independent and exponential processes. The primary goal of this paper is to obtain the optimum order quantity $\mathrm { Q ^ { * } }$ and the minimum total expected cost TEC(Q*).

Using matrix analysis, we generated a Markov process $\mathbf { Z } = ( \mathrm { N } , \mathrm { I } )$ for supply chain management and generated its stationary distributions, that is, for the probability functions P $( \mathrm { N } = \mathrm { n } , \mathrm { I } = \mathrm { j } )$ explicit expressions are obtained. We can read more structural information and perform profitable analysis to achieve our goal through the obvious shape of the steady-state distribution.

Different performance metrics are computed for the stationary system, and the total expected cost is calculated for a given dataset with varying $Q \in \{ 4 , 5 , . . . , 1 2 \}$ . The results are illustrated numerically indicating the optimum results, i.e., $\mathbf { Q } ^ { * }$ and TEC(Q*). All these ideas were also discussed for a case of independent queueing system and inventory system in IQIS.

As a concluding remark we hope to apply the proposed methodology further for various advanced types of queueing-inventory systems of G/G/c or M/G/c types operating under (s, S) inventory policies.

# Chapter 2 Nature-Inspired Optimization for Inventory Models with Imperfect Production

![](images/43cc0b5ff372bf3ac0d985f40647e459f791606c169bd3590550ad91786fc211.jpg)

Neetu Singh, Madhu Jain, and Praveendra Singh

Abstract Inventory management plays a crucial role in a production system, as such the system designer needs to design the inventory model which takes care of all aspects of the concerned system. It is evident that production systems unescapably face process deterioration, which is a significant concern for the system designers to ensure the proper planning of the concerned inventory systems. This paper presents a literature survey of studies on inventory models with imperfect production systems. A considerable amount of work has been done on this front as this is a realistic phenomenon encountered in the production systems. We have reviewed the prominent research on inventory models with imperfect production systems and nature-inspired optimization techniques. A brief discussion and review of literature on the nature-inspired optimization algorithms and their applications in inventory control are presented. The present article will be beneficial for the academicians and practitioners to get updated accounts of the works done on inventory models with imperfect production systems.

Keywords Imperfect production Inventory management Economic order quantity Nature Inspired Optimization

# 2.1 Introduction

Inventory theory plays a crucial role in production and operations management due to its suppleness to supervising manufacturing systems and, other industries,

N. Singh G L Bajaj Institute of Technology and Management, Greater Noida, India e-mail: drneetusinghmaths@gmail.com

M. Jain P. Singh (B) Indian Institute of Technology Roorkee, Roorkee, India e-mail: psingh@ma.iitr.ac.in

M. Jain e-mail: madhu.jain@ma.iitr.ac.in

and maximizing service level to the clients. Inventory models deal with the proper stock management to maximize the customer service. The revenue of any production system depends on various factors, including optimal inventory control. The total inventory cost needs to be minimized without affecting the customer service by maintaining proper inventory to fulfil the demand. For the balance between under stocking and over stocking, the appropriate planning of the concerned system is of great importance. The typical Economic Order Quantity (EOQ) and Economic Production Quantity (EPQ) models remain of great importance. Still, their use in real-time systems is limited as the assumptions are very simple and performed under ideal conditions. In EOQ inventory models, we study the optimized inventory level that minimizes the total costs. However, in EPQ models, the optimal production lot size under specified manufacturing environments is obtained. The inventory control and maintenance are the main issues of the product manufacturers and have attracted many researchers to analyze the production inventory systems.

There is a considerable amount of work available on different inventory models for the production systems. Several models are coming up to deal with the inventory control of real-time production systems. In a real-time manufacturing/production organization, the units produced may be defectives owing to diverse issues in the system. The challenging domains of the current age of progressive production environment are machine failure, imperfect quality products, quality deterioration and some unavoidable issues like labour walkouts, natural calamities, machine failures, resources shortage and transport issues, etc. More advanced inventory models are required to design real-time production systems. The work on imperfect production scenarios is increasing as it is a real phenomenon which greatly affects the customers’ service. Over the periods, frequent researches have been carried out to develop EPQ models which are more realistic by incorporating the imperfections of the process of production.

In this study, we have reviewed the research articles on inventory control of imperfect production systems by using nature-inspired optimization techniques. A brief introduction of some commonly used nature-inspired metaheuristics algorithms is provided. Some inventory studies which have used nature-inspired algorithms as an optimization tool are also presented. The current article is structured in different sections. An inventory model for the imperfect items is briefly described in Sect. 2.2. We have presented a survey of the literature on imperfect production by process deterioration in Sect. 2.3. A brief account of nature-inspired optimization (NIO) algorithms and some inventory studies via NIO algorithms are provided in Sects. 2.4 and 2.5, respectively. The important works on inventory management with imperfect production are reviewed in Sect. 2.6. At last, the highlights and scope of researches on the EPQ models are discussed in Sect. 2.7.

# 2.2 An Imperfect Production Inventory Model

In most of the researches on inventory, the units produced in a production system are perfect and can be stored for a long duration. In a real-time production system, a proportion of defectives generates during the production process, which is called an imperfect production system. Figure 2.1 shows the inventory level for a general imperfect production system (IPS). The produced items decay with a given deterioration rate. The production and demand rates are considered to be time dependent.

The notations used to formulate the stock level of the inventory are given below:

<table><tr><td>P(t):</td><td>Production rate</td></tr><tr><td>α:</td><td>Fraction of perfect produced items</td></tr><tr><td>D(t):</td><td>Demand rate</td></tr><tr><td>θ(t):</td><td>Deterioration rate</td></tr><tr><td>r(t):</td><td>Rework rate</td></tr><tr><td>Im:</td><td>Maximum inventory level</td></tr></table>

The differential equations governing the stock level are as follows:

$$
\frac {d I (t)}{d t} = \alpha P (t) - D (t) - \theta (t) I (t); 0 \leq t \leq T _ {1} \tag {2.1}
$$

$$
\frac {d I (t)}{d t} = r (t) - D (t) - \theta (t) I (t); T _ {1} \leq t \leq T _ {2} \tag {2.2}
$$

$$
\frac {d I (t)}{d t} = - D (t) - \theta (t) I (t); T _ {2} \leq t \leq T _ {3} \tag {2.3}
$$

![](images/998e0152f71cd0707a5a954c4a5efbb2ae5ce55197c6d0f7802cf29c5418b744.jpg)  
Fig. 2.1 Inventory level for an IPS with rework

subject to the initial and boundary conditions

$$
I (0) = 0, I \left(T _ {2}\right) = I _ {m}, I \left(T _ {3}\right) = 0 \tag {2.4}
$$

The various cost components concerned with the imperfect production inventory model can be obtained by using the stock level function I (t). A special case of the above model can be found in Widyadana and Wee (2012). The special case is obtained by considering $P ( t ) = P , \theta ( t ) { = } \theta , D ( t ) = d , r ( t ) = \alpha P$ .

# 2.3 Inventory Production Systems with Process Reliability

In the present competitive situation, production business administrators need to provide their clients best services. However, the production facilities are subject to deterioration due to ageing. Process reliability is a significant aspect of imperfect production situations, and the production system costs and profits are highly affected by it. It is almost impossible to have a system which produces all the perfect items. The classical EPQ model assumes that all the manufactured items are faultless, which is inaccurate for real-time manufacturing situations.

It is worthwhile to have a look at the EPQ models by considering the production process reliability (R) and demand (D). The function for the total cost (TC) incurred in the production cycle is given by.

TC Set-up cost Holding cost Production cost.

The production cost can be obtained as follows:

Production cost $= \mathrm { a } ( 1 { \cdot } \mathrm { R } ) ^ { \mathrm { b } } \mathrm { D } ^ { \mathrm { c } }$ , where a, b, and c are non-negative real numbers.

A summary of EPQ models is presented in Table 2.1. Kim et al. (2001) considered process deterioration in the EPQ model. Goyal and Cardenas-Barron (2002) proposed a generalization of the study considered by Salameh and Jaber (2000). Ben-Daya (2002) gave a unified model to study the preventive maintenance of EPQ with imperfect production and increasing hazard rate. Chung and Hou (2003) investigated the inventory model with process deterioration and process shifts and found the optimal production run length. Jamal et al. (2004) considered process deterioration with two rework policies. Lin et al. (2003) analyzed inventory management for the imperfect manufacturing systems considering periodic inspection plans and assuming that the defective objects are reworked before or after a sale.

Hsieh and Lee (2005) investigated inventory models to study a production system which produces a proportion of imperfect items. Wang (2005) recommended product inspections for the imperfect production system. Contrary to other works with full inspection policy to eliminate all the defective items, the inspections were performed when the production run ends to avoid inspection costs of a complete inspection. Rahim and Al-Hajailan (2006) examined imperfect production situations, including shortages and a time-dependent defective rate on the optimal production run. They
